<INPUT>CHAPTER XII.</INPUT>Alice's Evidence CHAPTER I. There was nothing so _very_ remarkable in that; nor did Alice think it so _very_ much out of the way to hear the Rabbit say to itself, "Oh dear! In another moment down went Alice after it, never once considering how in the world she was to get out again.

<INPUT>Either the well was very deep, or she fell very slowly, for she had plenty of time as she went down to look about her and to wonder what was going to happen next.</INPUT>"Well!" thought Alice to herself, "after such a fall as this, I shall think nothing of tumbling down stairs! <INPUT>Would the fall _never_ come to an end?</INPUT>"I wonder how many miles I've fallen by this time?" she said aloud. "I must be getting somewhere near the centre of the earth. "I wonder if I shall fall right _through_ the earth!

<INPUT>Do you think you could manage it?) "And what an ignorant little girl she'll think me for asking!</INPUT>No, it'll never do to ask: perhaps I shall see it written up somewhere." Down, down, down. There was nothing else to do, so Alice soon began talking again. "Dinah'll miss me very much to-night, I should think!" (Dinah was the cat.) "I hope they'll remember her saucer of milk at tea-time.

<INPUT>Dinah my dear!</INPUT>I wish you were down here with me!

<INPUT>Good-bye, feet!" (for when she looked down at her feet, they seemed to be almost out of sight, they were getting so far off).</INPUT>"Oh, my poor little feet, I wonder who will put on your shoes and stockings for you now, dears?

<INPUT>"You ought to be ashamed of yourself," said Alice, "a great girl like you," (she might well say this), "to go on crying in this way!</INPUT>Stop this moment, I tell you!" After a time she heard a little pattering of feet in the distance, and she hastily dried her eyes to see what was coming. the Duchess, the Duchess!

<INPUT>Alice took up the fan and gloves, and, as the hall was very hot, she kept fanning herself all the time she went on talking: "Dear, dear!</INPUT>How queer everything is to-day!
<INPUT>And yesterday things went on just as usual.</INPUT>I wonder if I've been changed in the night? Let me think: was I the same when I got up this morning?

<INPUT>I almost think I can remember feeling a little different.</INPUT>But if I'm not the same, the next question is, Who in the world am I? Ah, _that's_ the great puzzle!" <INPUT>"I'm sure I'm not Ada," she said, "for her hair goes in such long ringlets, and mine doesn't go in ringlets at all; and I'm sure I can't be Mabel, for I know all sorts of things, and she, oh!</INPUT>she knows such a very little!
<INPUT>Besides, _she's_ she, and _I'm_ I, and-oh dear, how puzzling it all is!</INPUT>I'll try if I know all the things I used to know.

<INPUT>Let me see: four times five is twelve, and four times six is thirteen, and four times seven is-oh dear!</INPUT>I shall never get to twenty at that rate! However, the Multiplication Table doesn't signify: let's try Geography.

<INPUT>"Fourteenth of March, I _think_ it was," he said.</INPUT>"Fifteenth," said the March Hare.

<INPUT>"Take off your hat," the King said to the Hatter.</INPUT>"It isn't mine," said the Hatter.

<INPUT>"I wish you wouldn't squeeze so," said the Dormouse, who was sitting next to her.</INPUT>"I can hardly breathe." "I can't help it," said Alice very meekly: "I'm growing." "You've no right to grow _here_," said the Dormouse. "It _began_ with the tea," the Hatter replied.
<INPUT>"Of course twinkling begins with a T!" said the King sharply.</INPUT>"Do you take me for a dunce? Go on!" "I'm a poor man," the Hatter went on, "and most things twinkled after that-only the March Hare said-" "I didn't!" the March Hare interrupted in a great hurry.

<INPUT>"You did!" said the Hatter.</INPUT>"I deny it!" said the March Hare. "After that," continued the Hatter, "I cut some more bread-and-butter-" "But what did the Dormouse say?" one of the jury asked. "That I can't remember," said the Hatter.
<INPUT>"You _must_ remember," remarked the King, "or I'll have you executed." <INPUT>(As that is rather a hard word, I will just explain to you how it was done.</INPUT>They had a large canvas bag, which tied up at the mouth with strings: into this they slipped the guinea-pig, head first, and then sat upon it.) <INPUT>"Come, that finished the guinea-pigs!" thought Alice.</INPUT>"Now we shall get on better." <INPUT>"Call the next witness!" said the King.</INPUT>The next witness was the Duchess's cook. She carried the pepper-box in her hand, and Alice guessed who it was, even before she got into the court, by the way the people near the door began sneezing all at once.

<INPUT>Imagine her surprise, when the White Rabbit read out, at the top of his shrill little voice, the name "Alice!" CHAPTER XII.</INPUT>"The trial cannot proceed," said the King in a very grave voice, "until all the jurymen are back in their proper places-_all_," he repeated with great emphasis, looking hard at Alice as he said so. <INPUT>"Nothing _whatever?_" persisted the King.</INPUT>"Nothing whatever," said Alice.

<INPUT>"That's very important," the King said, turning to the jury.</INPUT>"_Un_important, of course, I meant," the King hastily said, and went on to himself in an undertone, "important-unimportant-unimportant-important-" as if he were trying which word sounded best. <INPUT>At this moment the King, who had been for some time busily writing in his note-book, cackled out "Silence!" and read out from his book, "Rule Forty-two.</INPUT>_All persons more than a mile high to leave the court_." Everybody looked at Alice.
<INPUT>"_I'm_ not a mile high," said Alice.</INPUT>"You are," said the King.

There is much more difference as to their best work than in the case of those others who are loved "by allowance" by convention, and because it is felt to be the right and proper thing to love them.
<INPUT>And in the sect--fairly large and yet unusually choice--of Austenians or Janites, there would probably be found partisans of the claim to primacy of almost every one of the novels.</INPUT>On the other hand, I, for my part, declare for_ Pride and Prejudice _unhesitatingly.

<INPUT>The plot, though not elaborate, is almost regular enough for Fielding; hardly a character, hardly an incident could be retrenched without loss to the story.</INPUT>The elopement of Lydia and Wickham is not, like that of Crawford and Mrs. Rushworth, a_ coup de théâtre; _it connects itself in the strictest way with the course of the story earlier, and brings about the denouement with complete propriety. All the minor passages--the loves of Jane and Bingley, the advent of Mr.
<INPUT>Collins, the visit to Hunsford, the Derbyshire tour--fit in after the same unostentatious, but masterly fashion.</INPUT>To me this humour seems to possess a greater affinity, on the whole, to that of Addison than to any other of the numerous species of this great British genus. Spectator," and in Jane Austen's genius there was, though nothing mannish, much that was masculine. But the likeness of quality consists in a great number of common subdivisions of quality--demureness, extreme minuteness of touch, avoidance of loud tones and glaring effects.

<INPUT>Also there is in both a certain not inhuman or unamiable cruelty.</INPUT>Yet, both in Mr. Addison and in Miss Austen there is, though a restrained and well-mannered, an insatiable and ruthless delight in roasting and cutting up a fool. <INPUT>Musgrove's self-deceiving regrets over her son.</INPUT>And in that sense Miss Austen certainly was one. She may even have been one in the further sense that, like her own Mr.
<INPUT>Bennet, she took an epicurean delight in dissecting, in displaying, in setting at work her fools and her mean persons.</INPUT>I think she did take this delight, and I do not think at all the worse of her for it as a woman, while she was immensely the better for it as an artist._ _In respect of her art generally, Mr. And I am not so certain that I should myself use the word "narrow" in connection with her.

<INPUT>If her world is a microcosm, the cosmic quality of it is at least as eminent as the littleness.</INPUT>She does not touch what she did not feel herself called to paint; I am not so sure that she could not have painted what she did not feel herself called to touch. Even Scott required hill and mountain and ballad, even Coleridge metaphysics and German to enable them to chip the classical shell. The one was humanity, and the other was art.

<INPUT>Take almost any Frenchman, except the late M.</INPUT>de Maupassant, and watch him laboriously piling up strokes in the hope of giving a complete impression. You get none; you are lucky if, discarding two-thirds of what he gives, you can shape a real impression out of the rest.
<INPUT>But with Miss Austen the myriad, trivial, unforced strokes build up the picture like magic.</INPUT>Nothing is false; nothing is superfluous.
<INPUT>When (to take the present book only) Mr.</INPUT>Collins changed his mind from Jane to Elizabeth "while Mrs. Bennet was stirring the fire" (and we know_ how _Mrs.

<INPUT>Bennet would have stirred the fire), when Mr.</INPUT>Collins is really_ great; _far greater than anything Addison ever did, almost great enough for Fielding or for Swift himself.
<INPUT>It has been said that no one ever was like him.</INPUT>Collins was impossible or non-existent at the end of the eighteenth century. It is very interesting that we possess, in this same gallery, what may be called a spoiled first draught, or an unsuccessful study of him, in John Dashwood. The formality, the under-breeding, the meanness, are there; but the portrait is only half alive, and is felt to be even a little unnatural.

<INPUT>Collins is perfectly natural, and perfectly alive.</INPUT>Collins could not have been so satisfactorily exhibited if his creatress had not adjusted so artfully to him the figures of Mr. Bennet and of Lady Catherine de Bourgh.
<INPUT>The latter, like Mr.</INPUT>Collins himself, has been charged with exaggeration. There is, perhaps, a very faint shade of colour for the charge; but it seems to me very faint indeed.
<INPUT>Bennet, Miss Austen, and Mr.</INPUT>Darcy, and even Miss Elizabeth herself, were, I am inclined to think, rather hard on him for the "impropriety" of his conduct. <INPUT>It has sometimes, I believe, been urged that his pride is unnatural at first in its expression and later in its yielding, while his falling in love at all is not extremely probable.</INPUT>Here again I cannot go with the objectors.

<INPUT>When first Mr.</INPUT>Bennet had married, economy was held to be perfectly useless; for, of course, they were to have a son. This son was to join in cutting off the entail, as soon as he should be of age, and the widow and younger children would by that means be provided for.
<INPUT>Five daughters successively entered the world, but yet the son was to come; and Mrs.</INPUT>Bennet, for many years after Lydia's birth, had been certain that he would. <INPUT>Lady Catherine had been rendered so exceedingly angry by the contents of her nephew's letter, that Charlotte, really rejoicing in the match, was anxious to get away till the storm was blown over.</INPUT>At such a moment, the arrival of her friend was a sincere pleasure to Elizabeth, though in the course of their meetings she must sometimes think the pleasure dearly bought, when she saw Mr. Darcy exposed to all the parading and obsequious civility of her husband.
<INPUT>He bore it, however, with admirable calmness.</INPUT>He could even listen to Sir William Lucas, when he complimented him on carrying away the brightest jewel of the country, and expressed his hopes of their all meeting frequently at St. James's, with very decent composure. If he did shrug his shoulders, it was not till Sir William was out of sight.

<INPUT>Philips's vulgarity was another, and, perhaps, a greater tax on his forbearance; and though Mrs.</INPUT>Philips, as well as her sister, stood in too much awe of him to speak with the familiarity which Bingley's good-humour encouraged; yet, whenever she _did_ speak, she must be vulgar. Nor was her respect for him, though it made her more quiet, at all likely to make her more elegant. [Illustration] CHAPTER LXI.
<INPUT>[Illustration] Happy for all her maternal feelings was the day on which Mrs.</INPUT>Bennet got rid of her two most deserving daughters. With what delighted pride she afterwards visited Mrs. Bingley, and talked of Mrs.

<INPUT>Darcy, may be guessed.</INPUT>Bennet missed his second daughter exceedingly; his affection for her drew him oftener from home than anything else could do. He delighted in going to Pemberley, especially when he was least expected.
<INPUT>Bingley and Jane remained at Netherfield only a twelvemonth.</INPUT>So near a vicinity to her mother and Meryton relations was not desirable even to _his_ easy temper, or _her_ affectionate heart. Kitty, to her very material advantage, spent the chief of her time with her two elder sisters. In society so superior to what she had generally known, her improvement was great.
<INPUT>She was not of so ungovernable a temper as Lydia; and, removed from the influence of Lydia's example, she became, by proper attention and management, less irritable, less ignorant, and less insipid.</INPUT>From the further disadvantage of Lydia's society she was of course carefully kept; and though Mrs. Wickham frequently invited her to come and stay with her, with the promise of balls and young men, her father would never consent to her going. Mary was the only daughter who remained at home; and she was necessarily drawn from the pursuit of accomplishments by Mrs.

<INPUT>Bennet's being quite unable to sit alone.</INPUT>As for Wickham and Lydia, their characters suffered no revolution from the marriage of her sisters. The congratulatory letter which Elizabeth received from Lydia on her marriage explained to her that, by his wife at least, if not by himself, such a hope was cherished.
<INPUT>The letter was to this effect:-- /* "My dear Lizzy, */ "I wish you joy.</INPUT>If you love Mr.

<INPUT>Darcy half so well as I do my dear Wickham, you must be very happy.</INPUT>It is a great comfort to have you so rich; and when you have nothing else to do, I hope you will think of us. I am sure Wickham would like a place at court very much; and I do not think we shall have quite money enough to live upon without some help.
<INPUT>Any place would do of about three or four hundred a year; but, however, do not speak to Mr.</INPUT>Darcy about it, if you had rather not. "Yours," etc. As it happened that Elizabeth had much rather not, she endeavoured in her answer to put an end to every entreaty and expectation of the kind.

<INPUT>Such relief, however, as it was in her power to afford, by the practice of what might be called economy in her own private expenses, she frequently sent them.</INPUT>Their manner of living, even when the restoration of peace dismissed them to a home, was unsettled in the extreme. They were always moving from place to place in quest of a cheap situation, and always spending more than they ought.

<INPUT>His affection for her soon sunk into indifference: hers lasted a little longer; and, in spite of her youth and her manners, she retained all the claims to reputation which her marriage had given her.</INPUT>Though Darcy could never receive _him_ at Pemberley, yet, for Elizabeth's sake, he assisted him further in his profession. Pemberley was now Georgiana's home; and the attachment of the sisters was exactly what Darcy had hoped to see.
<INPUT>They were able to love each other, even as well as they intended.</INPUT>Georgiana had the highest opinion in the world of Elizabeth; though at first she often listened with an astonishment bordering on alarm at her lively, sportive manner of talking to her brother. He, who had always inspired in herself a respect which almost overcame her affection, she now saw the object of open pleasantry. Her mind received knowledge which had never before fallen in her way.
<INPUT>By Elizabeth's instructions she began to comprehend that a woman may take liberties with her husband, which a brother will not always allow in a sister more than ten years younger than himself.</INPUT>With the Gardiners they were always on the most intimate terms. Darcy, as well as Elizabeth, really loved them; and they were both ever sensible of the warmest gratitude towards the persons who, by bringing her into Derbyshire, had been the means of uniting them. <INPUT>The Adventure of the Copper Beeches I.</INPUT>A SCANDAL IN BOHEMIA I. To Sherlock Holmes she is always _the_ woman. I have seldom heard him mention her under any other name.

<INPUT>In his eyes she eclipses and predominates the whole of her sex.</INPUT>It was not that he felt any emotion akin to love for Irene Adler.
<INPUT>All emotions, and that one particularly, were abhorrent to his cold, precise but admirably balanced mind.</INPUT>He was, I take it, the most perfect reasoning and observing machine that the world has seen, but as a lover he would have placed himself in a false position. He never spoke of the softer passions, save with a gibe and a sneer. They were admirable things for the observer-excellent for drawing the veil from men's motives and actions.
<INPUT>Grit in a sensitive instrument, or a crack in one of his own high-power lenses, would not be more disturbing than a strong emotion in a nature such as his.</INPUT>And yet there was but one woman to him, and that woman was the late Irene Adler, of dubious and questionable memory.

<INPUT>I had seen little of Holmes lately.</INPUT>My marriage had drifted us away from each other. Beyond these signs of his activity, however, which I merely shared with all the readers of the daily press, I knew little of my former friend and companion.
<INPUT>One night-it was on the twentieth of March, 1888-I was returning from a journey to a patient (for I had now returned to civil practice), when my way led me through Baker Street.</INPUT>His rooms were brilliantly lit, and, even as I looked up, I saw his tall, spare figure pass twice in a dark silhouette against the blind.
<INPUT>He was pacing the room swiftly, eagerly, with his head sunk upon his chest and his hands clasped behind him.</INPUT>To me, who knew his every mood and habit, his attitude and manner told their own story. He was at work again. He had risen out of his drug-created dreams and was hot upon the scent of some new problem.

<INPUT>I rang the bell and was shown up to the chamber which had formerly been in part my own.</INPUT>His manner was not effusive. It seldom was; but he was glad, I think, to see me. With hardly a word spoken, but with a kindly eye, he waved me to an armchair, threw across his case of cigars, and indicated a spirit case and a gasogene in the corner.
<INPUT>Then he stood before the fire and looked me over in his singular introspective fashion.</INPUT>"Wedlock suits you," he remarked. "I think, Watson, that you have put on seven and a half pounds since I saw you." "Seven!" I answered. "Indeed, I should have thought a little more.
<INPUT>Just a trifle more, I fancy, Watson.</INPUT>And in practice again, I observe. You did not tell me that you intended to go into harness." "Then, how do you know?" "I see it, I deduce it.

<INPUT>How do I know that you have been getting yourself very wet lately, and that you have a most clumsy and careless servant girl?" "My dear Holmes," said I, "this is too much.</INPUT>You would certainly have been burned, had you lived a few centuries ago. It is true that I had a country walk on Thursday and came home in a dreadful mess, but as I have changed my clothes I can't imagine how you deduce it. As to Mary Jane, she is incorrigible, and my wife has given her notice, but there, again, I fail to see how you work it out." He chuckled to himself and rubbed his long, nervous hands together.

<INPUT>"It is simplicity itself," said he; "my eyes tell me that on the inside of your left shoe, just where the firelight strikes it, the leather is scored by six almost parallel cuts.</INPUT>Obviously they have been caused by someone who has very carelessly scraped round the edges of the sole in order to remove crusted mud from it.

<INPUT>Hence, you see, my double deduction that you had been out in vile weather, and that you had a particularly malignant boot-slitting specimen of the London slavey.</INPUT>And yet I believe that my eyes are as good as yours." "Quite so," he answered, lighting a cigarette, and throwing himself down into an armchair. "You see, but you do not observe. The distinction is clear.
<INPUT>For example, you have frequently seen the steps which lead up from the hall to this room." "Frequently." "How often?" "Well, some hundreds of times." "Then how many are there?" "How many?</INPUT>I don't know." "Quite so!
<INPUT>You have not observed.</INPUT>And yet you have seen.

<INPUT>That is just my point.</INPUT>Now, I know that there are seventeen steps, because I have both seen and observed. "It came by the last post," said he.

<INPUT>"Read it aloud." The note was undated, and without either signature or address.</INPUT>"There will call upon you to-night, at a quarter to eight o'clock," it said, "a gentleman who desires to consult you upon a matter of the very deepest moment. Your recent services to one of the royal houses of Europe have shown that you are one who may safely be trusted with matters which are of an importance which can hardly be exaggerated.
<INPUT>This account of you we have from all quarters received.</INPUT>Be in your chamber then at that hour, and do not take it amiss if your visitor wear a mask." "This is indeed a mystery," I remarked.

<INPUT>"What do you imagine that it means?" "I have no data yet.</INPUT>It is a capital mistake to theorise before one has data. Insensibly one begins to twist facts to suit theories, instead of theories to suit facts. But the note itself.
<INPUT>What do you deduce from it?" I carefully examined the writing, and the paper upon which it was written.</INPUT>"The man who wrote it was presumably well to do," I remarked, endeavouring to imitate my companion's processes.

<INPUT>"Such paper could not be bought under half a crown a packet.</INPUT>It is peculiarly strong and stiff." "Peculiar-that is the very word," said Holmes. "It is not an English paper at all. Hold it up to the light." I did so, and saw a large "E" with a small "g," a "P," and a large "G" with a small "t" woven into the texture of the paper.
<INPUT>"What do you make of that?" asked Holmes.</INPUT>"The name of the maker, no doubt; or his monogram, rather." "Not at all. "Eglow, Eglonitz-here we are, Egria. It is in a German-speaking country-in Bohemia, not far from Carlsbad.

<INPUT>"The paper was made in Bohemia," I said.</INPUT>"Precisely. And the man who wrote the note is a German. Do you note the peculiar construction of the sentence-'This account of you we have from all quarters received.' A Frenchman or Russian could not have written that.
<INPUT>It is the German who is so uncourteous to his verbs.</INPUT>It only remains, therefore, to discover what is wanted by this German who writes upon Bohemian paper and prefers wearing a mask to showing his face. <INPUT>"A pair, by the sound," said he.</INPUT>"Yes," he continued, glancing out of the window. "A nice little brougham and a pair of beauties. A hundred and fifty guineas apiece.

<INPUT>There's money in this case, Watson, if there is nothing else." "I think that I had better go, Holmes." "Not a bit, Doctor.</INPUT>Stay where you are. I am lost without my Boswell. And this promises to be interesting.

<INPUT>It would be a pity to miss it." "But your client-" "Never mind him.</INPUT>I may want your help, and so may he. Here he comes. <INPUT>Then there was a loud and authoritative tap.</INPUT>"Come in!" said Holmes. <INPUT>Boots which extended halfway up his calves, and which were trimmed at the tops with rich brown fur, completed the impression of barbaric opulence which was suggested by his whole appearance.</INPUT>From the lower part of the face he appeared to be a man of strong character, with a thick, hanging lip, and a long, straight chin suggestive of resolution pushed to the length of obstinacy. <INPUT>"I told you that I would call." He looked from one to the other of us, as if uncertain which to address.</INPUT>"Pray take a seat," said Holmes. "This is my friend and colleague, Dr.
<INPUT>Watson, who is occasionally good enough to help me in my cases.</INPUT>Whom have I the honour to address?" "You may address me as the Count Von Kramm, a Bohemian nobleman. <INPUT>If not, I should much prefer to communicate with you alone." I rose to go, but Holmes caught me by the wrist and pushed me back into my chair.</INPUT>"It is both, or none," said he.

<INPUT>"You may say before this gentleman anything which you may say to me." <INPUT>"The circumstances are of great delicacy, and every precaution has to be taken to quench what might grow to be an immense scandal and seriously compromise one of the reigning families of Europe.</INPUT>Our visitor glanced with some apparent surprise at the languid, lounging figure of the man who had been no doubt depicted to him as the most incisive reasoner and most energetic agent in Europe. <INPUT>Why should I attempt to conceal it?" "Why, indeed?" murmured Holmes.</INPUT>Yet the matter was so delicate that I could not confide it to an agent without putting myself in his power. <INPUT>The name is no doubt familiar to you." <INPUT>Born in New Jersey in the year 1858.</INPUT>Contralto-hum! La Scala, hum! Prima donna Imperial Opera of Warsaw-yes!
<INPUT>Retired from operatic stage-ha!</INPUT>Living in London-quite so! <INPUT>If this young person should produce her letters for blackmailing or other purposes, how is she to prove their authenticity?" "There is the writing." "Pooh, pooh!</INPUT>Forgery." <INPUT>You will find me at the Langham under the name of the Count Von Kramm." "Then I shall drop you a line to let you know how we progress." "Pray do so.</INPUT>"There are three hundred pounds in gold and seven hundred in notes," he said.

II.</INPUT>At three o'clock precisely I was at Baker Street, but Holmes had not yet returned. The landlady informed me that he had left the house shortly after eight o'clock in the morning.

<INPUT>I sat down beside the fire, however, with the intention of awaiting him, however long he might be.</INPUT>So accustomed was I to his invariable success that the very possibility of his failing had ceased to enter into my head. <INPUT>Accustomed as I was to my friend's amazing powers in the use of disguises, I had to look three times before I was certain that it was indeed he.</INPUT>With a nod he vanished into the bedroom, whence he emerged in five minutes tweed-suited and respectable, as of old. <INPUT>"What is it?" "It's quite too funny.</INPUT>I am sure you could never guess how I employed my morning, or what I ended by doing." "I can't imagine. I suppose that you have been watching the habits, and perhaps the house, of Miss Irene Adler." "Quite so; but the sequel was rather unusual.
<INPUT>I will tell you, however.</INPUT>I left the house a little after eight o'clock this morning in the character of a groom out of work. There is a wonderful sympathy and freemasonry among horsey men.
<INPUT>Be one of them, and you will know all that there is to know.</INPUT>I soon found Briony Lodge.

<INPUT>It is a _bijou_ villa, with a garden at the back, but built out in front right up to the road, two stories.</INPUT>Chubb lock to the door. <INPUT>I walked round it and examined it closely from every point of view, but without noting anything else of interest.</INPUT>"I then lounged down the street and found, as I expected, that there was a mews in a lane which runs down by one wall of the garden. <INPUT>So say the Serpentine-mews, to a man.</INPUT>She lives quietly, sings at concerts, drives out at five every day, and returns at seven sharp for dinner. Seldom goes out at other times, except when she sings.

<INPUT>Godfrey Norton, of the Inner Temple.</INPUT>See the advantages of a cabman as a confidant.

<INPUT>When a woman thinks that her house is on fire, her instinct is at once to rush to the thing which she values most.</INPUT>It is a perfectly overpowering impulse, and I have more than once taken advantage of it.

THE ADVENTURE OF THE BLUE CARBUNCLE I had called upon my friend Sherlock Holmes upon the second morning after Christmas, with the intention of wishing him the compliments of the season.

THE ADVENTURE OF THE ENGINEER'S THUMB Of all the problems which have been submitted to my friend, Mr. Sherlock Holmes, for solution during the years of our intimacy, there were only two which I was the means of introducing to his notice-that of Mr.

<INPUT>Hatherley's thumb, and that of Colonel Warburton's madness.</INPUT>At the time the circumstances made a deep impression upon me, and the lapse of two years has hardly served to weaken the effect. It was in the summer of '89, not long after my marriage, that the events occurred which I am now about to summarise. My practice had steadily increased, and as I happened to live at no very great distance from Paddington Station, I got a few patients from among the officials.
<INPUT>One of these, whom I had cured of a painful and lingering disease, was never weary of advertising my virtues and of endeavouring to send me on every sufferer over whom he might have any influence.</INPUT>One morning, at a little before seven o'clock, I was awakened by the maid tapping at the door to announce that two men had come from Paddington and were waiting in the consulting-room. <INPUT>"It's a new patient," he whispered.</INPUT>"I thought I'd bring him round myself; then he couldn't slip away. There he is, all safe and sound. I must go now, Doctor; I have my dooties, just the same as you." And off he went, this trusty tout, without even giving me time to thank him.

<INPUT>I entered my consulting-room and found a gentleman seated by the table.</INPUT>He was quietly dressed in a suit of heather tweed with a soft cloth cap which he had laid down upon my books. Round one of his hands he had a handkerchief wrapped, which was mottled all over with bloodstains.

<INPUT>"I am sorry to knock you up so early, Doctor," said he, "but I have had a very serious accident during the night.</INPUT>I came in by train this morning, and on inquiring at Paddington as to where I might find a doctor, a worthy fellow very kindly escorted me here. <INPUT>Victor Hatherley, hydraulic engineer, 16A, Victoria Street (3rd floor)." <INPUT>All my medical instincts rose up against that laugh.</INPUT>"Stop it!" I cried; "pull yourself together!" and I poured out some water from a caraffe.
